<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><i><b>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</b></i> is the second studio album by the English <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/Oasis_(band)" title="Oasis (band)">Oasis</a>, released on 2 October 1995 by <a href="/wiki/Creation_Records" title="Creation Records">Creation Records</a>. It was produced by <a href="/wiki/Owen_Morris" title="Owen Morris">Owen Morris</a> and the group's guitarist <a href="/wiki/Noel_Gallagher" title="Noel Gallagher">Noel Gallagher</a>. The structure and arrangement style of the album were a significant departure from the group's previous record <i><a href="/wiki/Definitely_Maybe" title="Definitely Maybe">Definitely Maybe</a></i>. Gallagher's compositions were more focused in balladry and placed more emphasis on huge choruses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oasis-recordinginfo.co.uk_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-oasis-recordinginfo.co.uk-1">[1]</a></sup> with the string arrangements and more varied instrumentation on the record contrasting with the rawness of the group's debut album. <i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i> was the group's first album with drummer <a href="/wiki/Alan_White_(Oasis_drummer)" title="Alan White (Oasis drummer)">Alan White</a>, who replaced <a href="/wiki/Tony_McCarroll" title="Tony McCarroll">Tony McCarroll</a>.</p>
<p>The record propelled Oasis from being a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crossover_(music)" title="Crossover (music)">crossover</a> indie act to a worldwide rock phenomenon, and according to various critics, was a significant record in the timeline of British <a href="/wiki/Independent_music" title="Independent music">indie music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seven_Ages_of_Rock.2C_What_the_World_is_Waiting_For_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seven_Ages_of_Rock.2C_What_the_World_is_Waiting_For-2">[2]</a></sup> The band's most commercially successful release, <i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i> sold a record-breaking 347,000 copies in its first week on sale, spent 10 weeks at number one on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Albums Chart</a>, and reached number four in the US <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>. Singles from the album were successful in Britain, America and Australia: "<a href="/wiki/Some_Might_Say" title="Some Might Say">Some Might Say</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Look_Back_in_Anger" title="Don't Look Back in Anger">Don't Look Back in Anger</a>" reached number one in the UK; "<a href="/wiki/Champagne_Supernova" title="Champagne Supernova">Champagne Supernova</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Wonderwall_(song)" title="Wonderwall (song)">Wonderwall</a>" reached number one on the US <a href="/wiki/Alternative_Songs" title="Alternative Songs">Modern Rock Tracks</a> chart; and "Wonderwall" topped the Australian and New Zealand singles charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although a commercial smash, the record received initially lukewarm reviews from mainstream music critics; many contemporary reviewers deemed it inferior to <i>Definitely Maybe</i>, with the songwriting and production particular points of criticism. In the ensuing years, however, critical opinion towards the album reversed, and it is now generally considered a seminal record of both the <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> era, and the 1990s in general. Over several months in 1995 and 1996, the band performed an extensive world tour in support of the album. The most notable of the concerts were two <a href="/wiki/Knebworth_House" title="Knebworth House">Knebworth House</a> performances in August to a combined crowd of 250,000 people. At the <a href="/wiki/1996_Brit_Awards" title="1996 Brit Awards">1996 Brit Awards</a>, the album won Best British Album.</p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/2010_Brit_Awards" title="2010 Brit Awards">2010 Brit Awards</a>, <i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i> was named the greatest British album since 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-4">[4]</a></sup> It has sold over 22 million copies worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and appears on several lists of the greatest albums in rock music. As of July 2016, it is the UK's fifth best-selling album of all time, having sold over 4.7 million copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UK_sales_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-UK_sales-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background_and_recording">Background and recording</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background and recording">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In May 1995, in the wake of the critical and commercial success of their 1994 debut album, <i><a href="/wiki/Definitely_Maybe" title="Definitely Maybe">Definitely Maybe</a></i>, Oasis began recording <i>Morning Glory</i> at <a href="/wiki/Rockfield_Studios" title="Rockfield Studios">Rockfield Studios</a> in Wales, with <a href="/wiki/Owen_Morris" title="Owen Morris">Owen Morris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noel_Gallagher" title="Noel Gallagher">Noel Gallagher</a> producing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MOJO_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-MOJO-7">[7]</a></sup> By the time they were finished in June 1995, Oasis were on the brink of becoming one of the most popular bands in the UK: the August 1995 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britpop" title="Battle of Britpop">Battle of Britpop</a> incident in which Oasis and <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a> had a chart battle over their singles "<a href="/wiki/Roll_with_It_(Oasis_song)" title="Roll with It (Oasis song)">Roll with It</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Country_House" title="Country House">Country House</a>", would propel them to mainstream awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The band recorded the album quickly: early on, averaging almost one song every twenty-four hours. However, tension arose between songwriter Noel Gallagher and his younger brother, lead singer <a href="/wiki/Liam_Gallagher" title="Liam Gallagher">Liam</a>, when Noel wanted to sing lead vocals on either "<a href="/wiki/Wonderwall_(song)" title="Wonderwall (song)">Wonderwall</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Don%27t_Look_Back_in_Anger" title="Don't Look Back in Anger">Don't Look Back in Anger</a>". The younger Gallagher considered this tantamount to a temporary exile from his own group. The issue dissipated momentarily as Noel was pleased with Liam's vocal take of "Wonderwall". However, tension returned due to Liam's strained attempts to sing the high notes on "<a href="/wiki/Champagne_Supernova" title="Champagne Supernova">Champagne Supernova</a>". When Noel subsequently took his turn to record his vocals for "Don't Look Back in Anger", Liam went to a local pub and came back accompanied by a crowd of people, including music journalist <a href="/wiki/John_Robb_(musician)" title="John Robb (musician)">John Robb</a> who was producing the band <a href="/wiki/Cable" title="Cable">Cable</a> in nearby studio Monnow Valley whilst recording was still underway. After an altercation with Cable that infuriated his brother, the siblings then began fighting viciously, the session was abandoned and recording was suspended.</p>
<p>When the Gallagher brothers were reconciled three weeks later, the group spent another two weeks working on the album, followed by post-production work in London. Despite the friction involved between the Gallagher brothers, Owen Morris reflected in 2010 that: "The sessions were the best, easiest, least fraught, most happily creative time I've ever had in a recording studio. I believe people can feel and hear when music is dishonest and motivated by the wrong reasons. <i>Morning Glory</i>, for all its imperfection and flaws, is dripping with love and happiness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oasis-recordinginfo.co.uk_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oasis-recordinginfo.co.uk-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Weller" title="Paul Weller">Paul Weller</a> joined them in the studio and provided lead guitar and backing vocals for "Champagne Supernova",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weller_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weller-10">[10]</a></sup> and harmonica for the two untitled tracks known as "The Swamp Song".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Noel wrote the last song for the album, "Cast No Shadow", on the train as he returned to the studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MOJO_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-MOJO-7">[7]</a></sup> Morris claimed the album was recorded in 15 days; when it was finished he said it would "wipe the field with any competition ... It's astonishing. It's the <i><a href="/wiki/Never_Mind_the_Bollocks,_Here%27s_the_Sex_Pistols" title="Never Mind the Bollocks, Here's the Sex Pistols">Bollocks</a></i> for this decade."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The brickwall <a href="/wiki/Audio_mastering" title="Audio mastering">mastering</a> technique utilised during the recording of the album has led to some journalists claiming that it was responsible for initiating the <a href="/wiki/Loudness_war" title="Loudness war">loudness war</a>, as its heavy use of <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_range_compression" title="Dynamic range compression">compression</a>, first widely used by Morris on <i>Definitely Maybe</i>, was leaps and bounds beyond what any other album up until then had attempted. Music journalist Nick Southall, who has written extensively on the loudness war, commented, "If there's a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jump_the_shark" title="Jump the shark">jump-the-shark</a> moment as far as CD mastering goes then it's probably Oasis."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In <i>Britpop and the English Music Tradition</i> Andy Bennet and John Stratton noted that as a result of this technique "the songs were especially loud. [Liam] Gallagher's voice is foregrounded to the point that it appears to grow out of the mixes of the songs, exposing itself to execute a pseudo-live quality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Harris_(critic)" title="John Harris (critic)">John Harris</a> commented in his music history <i>Britpop!: Cool Britannia and the Spectacular Demise of English Rock</i> that much of the music on <i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i> seemed to be "little more inspired than a string of musical hand-me-downs". Among the musical cues Harris noted on the album were Gary Glitter's "Hello, Hello I'm Back Again" ("Hello", Glitter was an influence on Britpop), the theme to the 1970s children's programme <i>You and Me</i> and The Beatles' "<a href="/wiki/While_My_Guitar_Gently_Weeps" title="While My Guitar Gently Weeps">While My Guitar Gently Weeps</a>" ("She's Electric"), and the influence of R.E.M's "<a href="/wiki/The_One_I_Love_(R.E.M._song)" title="The One I Love (R.E.M. song)">The One I Love</a>" on "Morning Glory". One song, "Step Out", bore such a close resemblance to the song "<a href="/wiki/Uptight_(Everything%27s_Alright)" title="Uptight (Everything's Alright)">Uptight (Everything's Alright)</a>" by Stevie Wonder that it was removed from the album shortly before release due to the threat of legal action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._253.E2.80.9354_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._253.E2.80.9354-15">[15]</a></sup> In <i>Britpop...</i>, Bennet and Stratton analysed Liam Gallagher's vocal style in significant detail, stressing its importance to the songs of the album; "[Liam's] Mancunian accent blends into a register and timbre that works the gestural contours of the melody and lyrics." Bennet and Stratton went on to conclude that Liam's 'over-personalized' style on songs such as "<a href="/wiki/Wonderwall_(song)" title="Wonderwall (song)">Wonderwall</a>" resulted in "a beautiful sense of sentimentality that bespeaks the despondency of a generation. This occurs through the narrative structure of the song, vocal production, and the conventions of the singer's cultural context."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Noel Gallagher summed up his own perspective on the album's aesthetic in an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> in 1995; "Whilst [<i>Definitely Maybe</i>] is about dreaming of being a pop star in a band, <i>What's the Story</i> is about actually <i>being</i> a pop star in a band."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The album has a notable anthemic theme to its songs, differing from the rawness and edged rock of <i>Definitely Maybe</i>. The use of string arrangements and more varied instrumentation in songs such as "Don't Look Back in Anger" and "Champagne Supernova" was a significant departure from the band's debut. This style had first been implemented by the band on their fifth single, "<a href="/wiki/Whatever_(Oasis_song)" title="Whatever (Oasis song)">Whatever</a>", released in December 1994. It was produced in conjunction with the London Symphony Orchestra, resulting in a much more pop-oriented and mellower sound; this would be the template that would come to define many of the songs on <i>What's the Story</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_sevenages_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_sevenages-18">[18]</a></sup> In the BBC documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Ages_of_Rock" title="Seven Ages of Rock">Seven Ages of Rock</a></i>, former NME chief editor Steve Sutherland noted that "with <i>Morning Glory</i>, [Noel] began to take seriously the notion of being the voice of a generation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_sevenages_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_sevenages-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cover">Cover</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cover">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The cover is a picture of two men passing each other on Berwick Str in London. The two men are London DJ <a href="/wiki/Sean_Rowley" title="Sean Rowley">Sean Rowley</a> and album sleeve designer <a href="/wiki/Brian_Cannon" title="Brian Cannon">Brian Cannon</a> (back to the camera). The album's producer Owen Morris can be seen in the background, on the left footpath, holding the album's master tape in front of his face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fotostrasse.com_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-fotostrasse.com-19">[19]</a></sup> The location was chosen because the street was a popular location for record shops at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fotostrasse.com_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-fotostrasse.com-19">[19]</a></sup> The cover cost £25,000 to produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotion">Promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Whilst "<a href="/wiki/Some_Might_Say" title="Some Might Say">Some Might Say</a>", a number one hit, had been released in April, the single chosen to directly precede the album's release was "<a href="/wiki/Roll_with_It_(Oasis_song)" title="Roll with It (Oasis song)">Roll with It</a>", planned for release on 14 August, six weeks before the album was due to hit the shelves. This was an unorthodox method for the time, contrasting the standard industry procedure of releasing the lead single three weeks before its parent album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a>'s management had become worried that this would hinder the chances of the group's forthcoming "<a href="/wiki/Country_House" title="Country House">Country House</a>" single reaching number one the following week. As a reaction, <a href="/wiki/Food_Records" title="Food Records">Food Records</a> pushed the release of "Country House" back a week and thus started what became known as '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Britpop" title="The Battle of Britpop">The Battle of Britpop</a>'.</p>
<p>The event triggered an unprecedented amount of exposure for both bands in national newspapers and on television news bulletins, supposedly symbolising the battle between the middle class of the south and the working class of the north. In the midst of the battle a <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">Guardian</a></i> newspaper headline proclaimed "Working Class Heroes Lead Art School Trendies". In the event "Country House" outsold "Roll with It" by 54,000, and topped the singles chart for a fortnight. Overall singles sales that week were up by 41 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In 2005, John Harris reflected on the importance of the event in popularising Britpop; "(as) Blur's "Country House" raced Oasis' "Roll with It" to the top of the charts, just about every voice in the media felt compelled to express an opinion on the freshly inaugurated age of Britpop."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>During a promotional interview in September, the month before the album was released, Noel spoke about the rivalry with <a href="/wiki/Damon_Albarn" title="Damon Albarn">Damon Albarn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alex_James_(musician)" title="Alex James (musician)">Alex James</a> from Blur, and was quoted in the 17 September edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> saying he hoped "the pair of them would catch AIDS and die because I fucking hate them two."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._250_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._250-23">[23]</a></sup> The quote caused a storm of controversy, with Noel having to write a letter of apology; he later confessed that "my whole world came crashing down in on me then".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._250_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._250-23">[23]</a></sup> However, in an interview with <i>The Guardian</i> in 2005, Blur's guitarist <a href="/wiki/Graham_Coxon" title="Graham Coxon">Graham Coxon</a> explained that he bore no malice towards Oasis. "At least they were outright about it. They weren't pretending to like us and then slagging us off, which is what we'd been used to. In that way, I quite appreciated them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>What's the Story</i> was released on 2 October 1995. The album sold quickly; the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mirror" title="Daily Mirror">Daily Mirror</a></i> reported the day after release that central London <a href="/wiki/HMV" title="HMV">HMV</a> stores were selling copies of the album at a rate of two per minute. At the end of the first week of sales, the album had sold a record-breaking 347,000 copies, making it (at the time) the second-fastest-selling album in British history, behind <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bad_(album)" title="Bad (album)">Bad</a></i>. After initially entering the UK charts at number one, it hovered around the top three for the rest of the year before initiating a six-week stay at the top in mid January, followed by a further three weeks at number one in March. In total, the album didn't leave the top three for an astonishing seven months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the fourth single from the album, "Wonderwall", hit the top ten in several countries, including stays at number one in Australia, New Zealand and Spain, and a peak at number eight in the US, the album began to enjoy prolonged international success. Eventually the album had a five-week run at the top of the Australian albums chart and an eight-week run at the top of the New Zealand albums chart before topping charts in Canada, Ireland, Sweden and Switzerland. The album was also making significant waves in the US market as well, thanks in part to the success of the "Wonderwall" and "<a href="/wiki/Champagne_Supernova" title="Champagne Supernova">Champagne Supernova</a>" singles on American modern rock radio. Both songs reached number one on the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_Songs" title="Alternative Songs">Modern Rock Chart</a> and stayed there for ten and five weeks respectively. By early 1996, <i>What's the Story</i> was selling 200,000 copies a week, eventually peaking at number four and being certified four times platinum by the end of the year for shipments of over four million units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tour">Tour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F_Tour" title="(What's the Story) Morning Glory? Tour">(What's the Story) Morning Glory? Tour</a></div>
<p>The band embarked on what would become a 103 show world tour in support of the album over a period of several months in 1995 and 1996. The tour started on 22 June 1995 with a pre-Glastonbury festival warm up gig at the 1,400 capacity Bath Pavilion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> which featured the debut of new drummer Alan White and several new songs off the album, and ended on 4 December 1996 at the 11,800 capacity Mayo Civic Centre in Rochester, Minnesota, USA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and included concerts at <a href="/wiki/Earls_Court_Exhibition_Centre" title="Earls Court Exhibition Centre">Earls Court</a> in November 1995 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiff_International_Arena" title="Cardiff International Arena">Cardiff International Arena</a> in March 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris-28">[28]</a></sup> The tour had many disruptions and cancellations due to Noel twice walking out of the group, and Liam pulling out of a US leg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-harris-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1995, bass player <a href="/wiki/Paul_McGuigan_(musician)" title="Paul McGuigan (musician)">Paul McGuigan</a> walked out on the group after a flurry of verbal abuse from Liam whilst doing interviews in Paris. 'Guigsy' cited nervous exhaustion as the reason for his departure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._255_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._255-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scott_McLeod_(bassist)" title="Scott McLeod (bassist)">Scott Mcleod</a> of The Ya-Yas was brought in as his replacement; though, despite playing a string of gigs with the band and appearing in the video for the "<a href="/wiki/Wonderwall_(song)" title="Wonderwall (song)">Wonderwall</a>" single, Mcleod was unable to adapt to the frenetic celebrity lifestyle, duly returning to Manchester halfway through an American promotional tour for the album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._255_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._255-29">[29]</a></sup> The band played a few dates, including an appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/Late_Show_with_David_Letterman" title="Late Show with David Letterman">Late Show with David Letterman</a></i>, as a four piece, before McGuigan was convinced to return for the group's <a href="/wiki/Earls_Court_Exhibition_Centre" title="Earls Court Exhibition Centre">Earls Court</a> shows in early November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._255_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._255-29">[29]</a></sup> When the band broke up for a brief time in late 1996, several US tour dates and the entire Australia and New Zealand leg had to be cancelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the band began to reach the peak of their popularity, several large open-air concerts were organized in the UK during 1996, including two gigs at <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City_F.C." title="Manchester City F.C.">Manchester City</a> football stadium <a href="/wiki/Maine_Road" title="Maine Road">Maine Road</a>, two nights at <a href="/wiki/Loch_Lomond" title="Loch Lomond">Loch Lomond</a> in Scotland, and two nights at <a href="/wiki/Concerts_at_Knebworth_House" title="Concerts at Knebworth House">Knebworth House</a> in front of a record 125,000 people each night; an event that would come to be acknowledged as the height of the Britpop phenomenon, with one journalist commenting; "(Knebworth) could be seen as the last great Britpop performance; nothing after would match its scale."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> At the time, the concerts were the biggest gigs ever held for a single band on UK soil, and to date remain the largest demand ever for a British concert; with reportedly over 2,500,000 applications for tickets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Earl's Court and Maine Road gigs were filmed and later released as the Oasis VHS/DVD <i><a href="/wiki/...There_and_Then" title="...There and Then">...There and Then</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stone_Album_Guide" title="The Rolling Stone Album Guide">The Rolling Stone Album Guide</a></i></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><span style="white-space:nowrap" title="5/5 stars"><img alt="5/5 stars" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" width="11"/><img alt="" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="108" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/11px-Star_full.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/17px-Star_full.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Star_full.svg/22px-Star_full.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield_et_al.2004598_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield_et_al.2004598-41">[41]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle"><i><a href="/wiki/Select_(magazine)" title="Select (magazine)">Select</a></i></td>
<td style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle">4/5<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><i>What's the Story</i> was released to lukewarm reviews from the mainstream music press. Many contemporary reviewers expressed disappointment at the album's perceived inferiority to <i>Definitely Maybe</i>, taking aim at the 'banal lyrics' and the unoriginal nature of the compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archivedmusicpress.files.wordpress.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-archivedmusicpress.files.wordpress.com-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent.co.uk_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent.co.uk-44">[44]</a></sup> David Cavanagh of <i><a href="/wiki/Q_(magazine)" title="Q (magazine)">Q</a></i> magazine said of the lyrics "They scan; they fill a hole; end of story. They [say] nothing much about anything."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._254_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._254-45">[45]</a></sup> Andy Gill of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> commented that "She's Electric" is laddism of a tiresomely generic kind [whilst] "Roll With It" is drab and chummy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent.co.uk_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-independent.co.uk-44">[44]</a></sup> Perhaps the most damning review came from David Stubbs of the now-defunct <i><a href="/wiki/Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker">Melody Maker</a></i>. Despite stating that "Some Might Say" was "the best single of the year", Stubbs went on to be critical of the album as a whole; "<i>What's the Story</i> [sounds] laboured and lazy. On this evidence, Oasis are a limited band ... they sound knackered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archivedmusicpress.files.wordpress.com_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-archivedmusicpress.files.wordpress.com-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._254_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._254-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a positive review, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s Jon Weiderhorn wrote that "<i>What's the Story</i> is more than a natural progression, it's a bold leap forward that displays significant musical and personal growth." Weiderhorn went on to note that the 'stormy' relationship between Liam and Noel proved to be one of the album's strengths; "tension and instability have been inherent traits of great rock teams ... for Oasis, the addition of shared genes gives their songs extra impact and dimension."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-rs-40">[40]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> said that the album shows Oasis pursuing "an altogether different direction; away from the conscience-free overloaded hedonism towards an understanding of its consequences".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NME_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NME-38">[38]</a></sup> The album finished 10th in the voting for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Village_Voice" title="The Village Voice">The Village Voice</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s annual <a href="/wiki/Pazz_%26_Jop" title="Pazz &amp; Jop">Pazz &amp; Jop</a> critics poll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i>Britpop!</i>, John Harris concluded that the initial negative reviews of the time missed the album's universal strengths. "Those who fussed about the music's more artful aspects were missing the point. The fact that [Noel's] songs contained so many musical echoes seemed to couch the album in an air of homely reassurance." Harris believed that the "ordinary" nature of some of the album's songs "turned out to be part of its deeply populist appeal".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._254_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._254-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rob_Sheffield" title="Rob Sheffield">Rob Sheffield</a>, writing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stone_Album_Guide" title="The Rolling Stone Album Guide">The Rolling Stone Album Guide</a></i> (2004), called the album "a triumph, full of bluster and bravado but also moments of surprising tenderness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESheffield_et_al.2004598_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheffield_et_al.2004598-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> was less enthusiastic in his retrospective review and gave it a two-star honorable mention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000231_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000231-47">[47]</a></sup> indicating a "likable effort that consumers attuned to its overriding aesthetic or individual vision may well enjoy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000xvi_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000xvi-48">[48]</a></sup> He cited "She's Electric" and "Roll with It" as highlights and quipped "give them credit for wanting it all—and (yet another Beatles connection!) playing guitars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000231_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristgau2000231-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of today the tide of critical opinion has generally turned, and <i>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</i> is considered to be a seminal record of the <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a> era and as one of the best albums of the nineties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rollingstone.com_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-rollingstone.com-49">[49]</a></sup> and it appears in several charts as one of the greatest albums of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In 2010, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> commented that "the album is a triumph, full of bluster, bravado and surprising tenderness. <i>Morning Glory</i> capped a true golden age for Britpop."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The magazine ranked the album at 378 on its 2012 list of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_500_Greatest_Albums_of_All_Time" title="The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time">The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The album's enduring popularity within the UK was reflected when it won the BRITs Album of 30 years at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_BRIT_Awards" title="2010 BRIT Awards">2010 BRIT Awards</a>. The award was voted by the public to decide the greatest 'Best Album' winner in the history of the BRIT Awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The album was also included in the book <i><a href="/wiki/1001_Albums_You_Must_Hear_Before_You_Die" title="1001 Albums You Must Hear Before You Die">1001 Albums You Must Hear Before You Die</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>What's the Story</i> went on to become the second-best-selling album of 1995 and 1996 in the UK, as well being the best-selling album of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Its fourteen platinum certifications from the <a href="/wiki/British_Phonographic_Industry" title="British Phonographic Industry">British Phonographic Industry</a> were the highest ever awarded to a single record until <a href="/wiki/Adele" title="Adele">Adele</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/21_(Adele_album)" title="21 (Adele album)">21</a></i>, released in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The success of the album resulted in Oasis becoming one of the biggest bands in the United Kingdom, with substantial and considerable press coverage in the mainstream music press and frequent comparisons to <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> in the media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rollingstone.com_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-rollingstone.com-49">[49]</a></sup> Liam and Noel Gallagher both featured prominently in gossip columns and daily tabloids throughout 1996–97, their celebrity wives in <a href="/wiki/Patsy_Kensit" title="Patsy Kensit">Patsy Kensit</a> and Meg Matthews only heightening their popularity with British paparazzi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._327_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._327-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>What's the Story</i> propelled Oasis from being a crossover indie act to a worldwide rock phenomenon after the momentum gained by the critically acclaimed <i>Definitely Maybe</i>. It has been pinpointed by music critics as a significant record in the timeline of British indie music, demonstrating just how far into the mainstream independent music had ventured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seven_Ages_of_Rock.2C_What_the_World_is_Waiting_For_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seven_Ages_of_Rock.2C_What_the_World_is_Waiting_For-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2005, John Harris noted the significance of the album and "Wonderwall" in particular to Britpop's legacy. "When (Oasis) released Wonderwall, the rules of British music were decisively changed. From hereon in, the lighter-than-air ballad became obligatory, and the leather-trousers era of rock'n'roll was over."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian.co.uk_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-guardian.co.uk-8">[8]</a></sup> The success of the album in Britain resulted in Oasis becoming a cultural ubiquity for a brief period, featuring in tabloid newspapers on an almost daily basis and breaking sales records for live concerts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harris.2C_p._230_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harris.2C_p._230-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, while reviewing the most overrated albums of all time, where <i>Morning Glory</i> ranked number 16, writer Max Easton of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FasterLouder" title="FasterLouder">FasterLouder</a> wrote that the album is "full of anthems for the sake of anthems," and that "they're all variations on only a couple of good ideas." He concluded by saying, "<i>What’s the Story'</i>s lasting place in ‘90s folklore is less about the quality of the album, and more about the celebrity status and faux imagery attached to it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Track_listing">Track listing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Track listing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<th class="description" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: darkseagreen"><a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">Oasis</a> box set chronology</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<table style="width:100%; line-height: 1.4em; vertical-align: top">
<tr>
<td style="width: 33%; padding: 0.2em 0.1em 0.2em 0; text-align: center;"><i><a href="/wiki/Definitely_Maybe#Singles_box_set" title="Definitely Maybe">Definitely Maybe</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Box_set" title="Box set">Box set</a>)<br/>
(1996)</td>
<td style="width: 33%; padding: 0.2em 0.1em 0.2em 0.1em; text-align: center;"><i><b>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</b></i> (Box set)<br/>
(1996)</td>
<td style="width: 33%; padding: 0.2em 0 0.2em 0.1em; text-align: center;"></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>

</table>
<p>The <i><b>(What's the Story) Morning Glory?</b></i> <a href="/wiki/Box_set" title="Box set">box set</a> was released on 4 November 1996, featuring four discs of singles, including B-sides, and one disc of interviews. The album charted at number 24 on the <a href="/wiki/UK_Albums_Chart" title="UK Albums Chart">UK Albums Chart</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>All songs written by Noel Gallagher, except "<a href="/wiki/Cum_On_Feel_the_Noize" title="Cum On Feel the Noize">Cum On Feel the Noize</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Noddy_Holder" title="Noddy Holder">Noddy Holder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lea_(musician)" title="Jim Lea (musician)">Jim Lea</a>; "Step Out" co-written by <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cosby" title="Henry Cosby">Henry Cosby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Moy" title="Sylvia Moy">Sylvia Moy</a>.</p>

<td style="padding-right:10px;text-align:right;vertical-align:top">5:09</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_reissue">2014 reissue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2014 reissue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of a promotional campaign entitled <b>Chasing the Sun</b>, the album was re-released on 29 September 2014. The 3-disc deluxe edition includes remastered versions of the album and its associated b-sides from the four UK singles. Bonus content includes 5 demo tracks, and live choices taken from the band's iconic gigs at Earls Court, Knebworth Park and Maine Road.</p>
<p>All songs written by Noel Gallagher, except "<a href="/wiki/You%27ve_Got_to_Hide_Your_Love_Away" title="You've Got to Hide Your Love Away">You've Got to Hide Your Love Away</a>" by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lennon/McCartney" title="Lennon/McCartney">Lennon/McCartney</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=(What%27s_the_Story)_Morning_Glory%3F&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Oasis</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Liam_Gallagher" title="Liam Gallagher">Liam Gallagher</a> – lead vocals, <a href="/wiki/Tambourine" title="Tambourine">tambourine</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Noel_Gallagher" title="Noel Gallagher">Noel Gallagher</a> – lead and <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_guitar" title="Acoustic guitar">acoustic guitar</a>, vocals (lead on "Don't Look Back in Anger" and "Bonehead's Bank Holiday"), bass guitar, piano, <a href="/wiki/Mellotron" title="Mellotron">mellotron</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-bow" title="E-bow">e-bow</a>, production</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Arthurs" title="Paul Arthurs">Paul "Bonehead" Arthurs</a> – rhythm and acoustic guitar, piano, mellotron, drunk vocals on "Bonehead's Bank Holiday"</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_McGuigan_(musician)" title="Paul McGuigan (musician)">Paul McGuigan</a> – bass guitar</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alan_White_(Oasis_drummer)" title="Alan White (Oasis drummer)">Alan White</a> – drums, percussion (except on "<a href="/wiki/Some_Might_Say" title="Some Might Say">Some Might Say</a>")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tony_McCarroll" title="Tony McCarroll">Tony McCarroll</a> – drums on "Some Might Say"</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Additional musician</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Weller" title="Paul Weller">Paul Weller</a> – lead guitar and backing vocals on "Champagne Supernova" and harmonica on "Untitled" (excerpt 1 and 2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weller_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weller-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Additional personnel</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Owen_Morris" title="Owen Morris">Owen Morris</a> – production</li>
<li>Neil Dorfsman – <a href="/wiki/Audio_mixing_(recorded_music)" title="Audio mixing (recorded music)">multichannel mixing (SACD version)</a></li>
<li>David Swope – assistant mixing (SACD version)</li>
<li>Barry Grint – original audio mastering at <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">Abbey Road Studios</a> (now at Alchemy Soho)</li>
<li>Vlado Meller – <a href="/wiki/Audio_mastering" title="Audio mastering">mastering</a> (SACD version)</li>
<li>Michael Spencer Jones – photography</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brian_Cannon" title="Brian Cannon">Brian Cannon</a> – <a href="/wiki/Cover_art" title="Cover art">artwork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graphic_design" title="Graphic design">design</a></li>
<li>Mathew Sankey – assistant design</li>
</ul>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="background: #EEEEEE;">Studio albums</th>
